Hi Matt! I liked this segment that you did. You covered a portion of the fire problem that exists in the Wildland Urban Interface that we as fire professionals call Passive Fire Resistance or systems that are built into a structure to resist the heat of ignition. However, heat is just one leg of the 3 sided Fire Triangle. When Heat - Fuel and Oxygen come together they create a chemical chain reaction known as Fire. Take just one leg away and the Fire goes away. Fire rated glass, fire resistant concrete, non-combustible shingles all fall under the category ‚ÄúPassive Fire Resistance.‚Äù In 2011, I authored an article in Fire Chief magazine called ‚ÄúPreemptive Strike‚Äù where I introduce the concept of the Fire Prevention Triangle.‚Äù In the article I explain that fires can be prevented in 3 specific ways.  1. Active Fire Suppression - Fire sprinkler systems, Fire extinguishers or anything that is built into a building that automatically or manually aids in the extinguishment of a fire. 2. Passive Fire Resistance - 1 hour, 2 hour, 3 hour fire doors or fire walls, non-combustible fencing, siding or roofing or anything that is built to withstand the heat of ignition in a fire. 3. Early Fire Detection - Smoke detectors, Heat detectors, Inside pull stations or outside fire alarm boxes that summon the fire department and alert occupants to evacuate without delay. After 35+ years of fighting wind driven fires in Chicago I have come to the conclusion that the missing side of the fire prevention triangle in wildfires is #1 Active Fire Suppression. I believe more buildings could have been saved in LA if homeowners were more prepared to defend in place using advanced fire suppression techniques to keep the combustible portions of their property too wet to burn. There were many stories of people who stayed behind to hose down the dry grasses, and combustible siding, fencing, and roofing which kept flying embers at bay which prevented their home from burning. Make no mistake, they put themselves at risk, but risk is  a given in every fire.Just think how many more homes could have been saved if people prepared in advance to turn on roof top sprinklers or flow a nozzle solution of Class A Wetting Agent from a 55 gallon rain barrel on to their grass, bushes, siding and roof?  All fires start small and anything that‚Äôs wet can‚Äôt burn. Chief Hughes Battalion 1 (retired) Chicago FD

I know a builder in the area who built a few of the homes that burned down and had over engineered it for wildfires. He found that no matter how resistant you build the home, the fire can still get in through the windows due to the intense heat, causing them to crack. It makes sense as the fire itself can reach 2,000 degrees Fahrenheit. The larger the windows, the more susceptible they are to cracking. Then the embers fly in and ignite the interior of the home. Unless you are rebuilding with some type of fireproof window shutter, these homes are likely just going to burn down again.

The 1967 Hobart Tasmania fires were as selective as that. My grandparents at the top of a metro street geting close to the bush. Normal [for Tassy] timber home with timber sheds at the back. Homes both sides went, and across the road. But theirs was ok but a little singed. Others in the same area were similar, no rhyme or reason. And yes hot heavy winds burnt a LOT of area. My aunts home in the bush was only the brick chimney left,, and the main part of the [old] home was stone with kitchen etc made from wood at the rear . I have seen this here in South Oz quite a bit after fires.  And it seems that we are quite a bit ahead of LA and California on fire prevention and fighting. Again Govt apathy and greenies it could be better.  My father chased the 1939 South Australian bushfires for a week,, armed with little more than a wet wheat bag [jute] After that a Emergency Fire Brigade was created,, in many areas. Originally they had a trailer with a tank and over the back brass military knapsacks. And yes still some bags. Small step forward. This was all funded by the populace, raffles and similar fund raising for many years. After the war [ yeah got in the way of a lot]  they stepped up to a prewar Ford V8 6 ton truck with a big tank, a proper pump and long hoses. By this time the Govt handed out a bit of money and they also had a decent shed and land to store it all. Yes the trailer and those bags were still around! By the early 70s when I was a member they had had to move for roadworks,, [ a couple of times] so a new bigger shed with far more room for equipment and some formal training once a week. And we also had a Landcruiser tray top all kitted out which was faster for small fires or first responded. Then we got a 6 ton brand new funded by the Govt Dodge. 318 5 speed. Bigger water tank again. Better pumps, better everything. BUT it did not like backing down gulleys/ gorges so that was the domain [still] of the Ford. We lived in a hilly area with in the greater area some river gorges and just gulleys. By this time we were only semi rural with more and more estates popping up every year. By the mid 70s a lot was changing after  the experience of fires, fire fighting in general. The biggest was the [slow] adoption of 4wd and diesel engine trucks. Petrol vapourised in the heat [happened to us] and some trucks and crews were burnt. By this time I was no longer a member,,, though during business hours [when all the volounteers were at work] myself and father or his worker still put out small fires, car fires and the more regular home fires. I did a few by myself. Got on TV extinguishing a VW [yeah catch fire often] by myself on the side of the main highway through the area. By that time Metro fire service were coming into the area. Not enough ofcourse though they got the house fires which they were good at. Bloody useless though at a grass fire! Fires and firefighting is a totally different scenario than pre war these days. My father was still a member until his early 80s, he operated the radio from the firestation and coordinated brigades. In my time [1954] there was fire sirens to call out members,, my grandmother [the other side]  used to test the siren every morning at 8am on the dot. Ex air raid sirens, very loud. Later we had a second one at the shed a mile or so away. You had to know the times as you could hear 3 or 4 other brigades tests as well.  Over about a 12 mile radius. And if it was not those times you opened your eyes for smoke rising. How much of this has California got?? From what I gather only a smal amount and even forsetry or national parks are ill equiiped. We used to both raise revenue and fire management by charging block owners to burn off their blocks. And burning off for both the reservoir property and the council on their lands and on occasion road side burns as well. Fires often start on roadsides, dodgey vehicles, cigarette butts, and fire bugs. Anyone not that bright [Cal Gov]  should be locked up! As part of my fathers business we also did block slashing or rotary hoeing for those that did not want them burnt. Though slashed long grass can catch fire without too much effort,, but a LOT easier to put out. Neighbours with garden hose on occasion

Why is it that we're not builiding with autoclaved aerated concrete (AAC) blocks? This may have prevented much of the destruction, as this building material is far more fireproof than wood (and as opposed to concrete, which is not suitable due to earthquakes, AAC blocks better withstand earthquakes due to their lightweight, design flexibility). Homes and businesses constructed of AAC have been through nature's worst throughout the rest of the world for years - proof positive 100+ years now - isn't it about time that we implement this construction process in the States? We all know of successful sites here and there throughout the US, but not on any broad scope, but the benefits are far-reaching (including cool in summer, warm in winter, non-allergenic), and considering this most recent tragic event, the time is long past due for serious consideration.

Most countries in the world use much thicker walls than we do here in the U.S., often 2-3 feet thick, and most often made of masonry, concrete, or earthen walls.   What looks like wood paneling on the front of the home @ 7:30 is likely a manufactured wood panel, such as hemp-wood, bamboo-wood, laminated veneer, or even a metal panel with a synthetic wood finish.   As far as the demonstration @ 4:00, the use of magnesium sheathing would make those walls virtually fireproof up to 1400 degrees Farenheit.  There are a lot of attractive and inexpensive solutions to making homes more fire-resistant, as well as being more energy-efficient and sound-resistant.  The problem is most Americans want their home built as cheaply as legally possible, with 6" new-growth timber framing, weak gypsum drywall sheathing, cheap wood fencing, and terrible fiberglass insulation.  Until home buyers are ready to build for quality and value, instead of just for price, nothing will change.

This reminds me of the great Chicago fire of 1871. At that time, almost every building in Chicago was made of wood. The city has the Chicago River that divided the city into a few zones, that were thought to keep the fire from jumping the river. It turned out that the wind carried embers over the river and set fire to most of the city. Residents fled into the river, or Lake Michigan, to escape the flames. The old water tower (still in Chicago on Michigan Avenue) survived because it was made of stone. The city fire department was in a building made of masonry, so it was thought to be fire-proof. But, they made the roof out of wood, which caught fire, and then burned out the interior. Some other large buildings were also made of stone or brick, and were thought to be resistant to fire. But they all had wooden roofs that caught fire and then burned everything inside. It also turned out that the buildings had doors and windows made of wood, that caught fire, and transmitted the flames to the interior.

This might be the most important video that Matt ever made. California adopted special building codes for fire hazard areas in 2006 and enforced them for new construction after 2008. Some of what Matt was describing are covered by these codes. Unfortunately for California, that still leaves the fast majority of the housing stock in fire hazard area non compliant with current fire hazard codes. For new construction, making code compliant structures adds marginally to the cost of construction, but updating older construction to be fully code compliant can be very expensive. There is a middle ground though for updating older housing. A lot of fire resistance can be obtained with fairly inexpensive changes.   Part of the solution for California involves making homeowners more aware of the dangers they face and the possibility of affordable solutions. Matt's video is exactly the kind of thing that will help address awareness, Thank you Matt.

Greeting from Germany. I grew up in the south of France where wild fires are pretty common. So, the only thing I can bring to the table is how we do things.  1. Keeping your grounds clear of brush. Our regulations state that the owner has to clear the brush in a 100 meter / yard radius around the property. IF you don't you get fined pretty harshly. 2. No trees in the vicinity of the house that overtop your property's roof line. 3. Our building regulations state that the exterior walls of any building need to have a 60 minute fire resistance rating. 4. Traditionally, our houses are build with non-flammable materials, mostly natural stone, concrete blocks or terracotta blocks. The roofs are almost always made of terracotta tiles.  5. To protect against wind and heat most of our windows have exterior window shutters, mostly made out of 1 inch thick timber, but can also be steel. That's a design choice really.  As a side note, now that I work in Luxembourg, all new buildings here are required to have an energy rating of A, which is basically a passive house. That also means that all of the air control is made through a ventilation system, and as we saw with your second example. The fact that the only way for embers to enter the skin of the house is through a mechanical system that can be closed off is a big factor. In Europe the roof (and also windows) is the usual point of entry for fire, remove that possibility and you have a pretty good chance of saving your property.  Another point is that could be important is that we overwhelmingly use aluminium for window frames. We do also use wood/alu frames but the exterior face will always be metal.   After that, there are always systems that you can implement, but that really depends on how deep your pockets are. Tempered glas is one such solution if you don't want shutters.  But, although these solutions contribute to a safer more resistant building, they are in no way a guarantee that fire will spare your home. There are a lot of variables to account for and building with all those in mind is either incredibly expensive or downright impossible. The best solution maybe to let the forests burn every 4-5 years to keep the fuel low and promote regeneration. I don't think nature is so weak that it needs protection, except maybe from us.  I wish the people in California much courage and to rebuilt smartly !

My wife and I recently designed a home in north east Minnesota where there is high fire risk annually. It is highly recommended that properties have whole property fire suppression systems in addition to homes being designed for fires. There are mandatory 20 foot set backs from any building to the tree line. In 2022 there was an evacuation notice for the area and all fire suppression systems turned on by volunteer firefighters. Luckily the area did not burn, but an evac notice is a scary wakeup call to being prepared. We have yet to build our home so all of these lesson are helping us to not cut corners when it comes to wild fire preparedness.

Some info from a native SoCal framer/builder:  You spoke of the unusual wind directions at the beginning of the video and, yeah, the Santa Anas go the opposite direction of the onshore winds, which are the usual wind direction.  It comes from a high over the Four Corner states with the wind flowing to a low off the SoCal coast.  The air is compressed as it flows down the face of the San Gabriel and San Bernardino mountains increasing the temperatures dramatically.  The beach areas often experience warmer temps than the low deserts at the same time.  This was arguably the most intense Santa Anas I've ever seen as a native-born southern Californian of 67 years, and I've never seen or heard of 100mph+ Santa Anas.  It was crazy.  I remember dozens of palm trees taken down at the beach 45 years ago by Santa Anas winds, and last week winds were much faster.  The winds are usually faster on the west- and south-facing slopes of the mountains, so 100mph winds at the beach is just nuts.  I also just retired as a builder/developer/carpenter of 45 years and that one house that survived has been in the local news damn near every day.  Stucco is a great way to protect a house but, as you mentioned in your video, you gotta have appropriate windows and roofing, or the stucco won't matter.  I grew up in the local mountains (Lake Arrowhead) and fires are on our minds every single day of the year.  More and more people are going to concrete tile roofs but in a big wind it won't matter without top-notch underlayment.  Exterior insulation is a GREAT way of providing protection.  Pain in the ass for the framer and builder but screw 'em.  :-)  Eave vents are not allowed in wildfire/forest fire areas of California, so we got that right.  7/8" stucco qualifies as one-hour.  EIFS works, too, under some situations.  One-hour ratings only go in one direction, meaning, as an example, the type of insulation will not matter if you have stucco on the outside or 5/8" Type X on the inside, though something like rock wool will help with draft stopping.  Agree entirely with your steel framing deck.  Edit:  Forgot to add that most comp shingles are Class A.  Class A comp shingles are almost worthless in fires such as these (hence several lucky homeowners saying their houses survived due to pure luck).  You need metal or concrete with the appropriate underlayment to survive.  I've seen too many houses burn here in SoCal to say otherwise.  Great info in your video.
I think any area prone to fires should probably have stone patios instead of wooden decks.

"Writer Bruno Bettelheim, in his book The Uses of Enchantment, interprets the tale as a showcase of the capacity for anticipation and courage in the face of adversity, symbolized by the wolf. According to him, the individual who is content to prepare themself as the first two pigs will be destroyed by the vicissitudes of life, and only a person who builds a solid base can face such hazards. He viewed the tale as a means of telling children that one cannot always act according to the pleasure principle, and must submit to the reality principle when life demands it. He exemplified this point by observing that the first two pigs valued gratification rather than planning and foresight as the third pig had."  -- Analysis from https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Three_Little_Pigs

in 2018 here in Monchique Portugal  when the wildfires came through, although only 50- 60 km per hour winds, we lost some outbuildings but our 100yr old concrete/brick house stayed intact!  however amongst the losses  were 4 refurbished alloy wheels with new tyres, all that was left were runs of melted alloy, nothing else, nor even the tyre wires! ..Chainsaws and strimmers  disappeared,  steel wheelbarrows gone!   We live in the country so no close neighbours, but we are obliged to keep 5 mtrs clear around our house, possibly concreted all around which we had, and no flammable trees within 25 mtrs of your house! When you look at Pacific Palisades in some of the aerial shots it's obvious that the houses are too close to each other. It's not really surprising how quickly they caught on fire.   As a Product Designer I often look at problems from a different angle to most. What is noticeably self evident, is that 'cookie cutter' houses really don't stand up to the ravages of fire or earthquakes. In particular, asphalt shingles roofing. Here in the UK roofs are traditionally clad in slate tiles. These last considerably longer than asphalt shingles, and are also both more aesthetically, whilst more importantly, fire proof. I dare say they will be the more expensive option, but if losing your house is the other alternative, then the slates win in my books.   Some radical design and architectural changes are needed for future building, to include both fire proofing and earthquake proofing. When the Paradise fires hit California, I sent an email to Gavin Newsom suggesting an idea for an independent self contained sprinkler and fire suppression system to be fitted to roofs. As you can imagine, they weren't interested.

I am a German and living decades in a America.  My entire Germany living professional family history consists of graduate engineers, architects, including a construction company with over 100 years of experience.   What you are explaining, Mr. Risinger, is absolutely correct.   We in Europe, especially in Germany, Austria and France, have the highest rules in fire protection and building regulations, this is due to history because of many wars and firestorms after bombings.   Houses must be spaced at least 4 meters or 12 feet apart, with one Level Houses, more levels more distance.   Fire protection zones around the house made of walls or fire-resistant materials and fire-resistant outer walls of the houses made of stone or concrete, roofs must be made of at least fire-resistant materials, preferably metal roofs, tiled roofs or concrete roofs, but interior walls must also be fire-resistant....   There are also technical fire protection separation zones in town planning so that fires cannot spread over large areas.   Water systems for the fire hydrants are separate from the normal water supply system for the population.    In Los Angeles the authorities did pretty much everything wrong.   I spoke to those responsible behind closed doors.   Los Angeles has 2 water reservoirs, but they have been calling for repairs for years because they are defective.   And then there is only 1 reservoir.   The water supply has not really grown with the city.   In fact, 7 water reservoirs are needed.   But politicians have been rejecting this for decades.   Then there was a water shortage because the reservoir was empty because a lot of water was needed. How did this happen.....   Terrible but simply the result of political mistakes.   The reservoirs are supplied with water via pumps from rivers and soil, but one of the rivers was diverted for green reasons and flows unused into the Pacific because a rare species of fish is to be re-established in this river.   The result was that the tanks could not be refilled quickly enough.   Another reason in the city planning was that new fire protection corridors were rejected and existing ones were released for building land.    The result was that the fire was no longer controllable, particularly because of the winds and firestorms caused by the fires.   This meant that the fire was able to spread unhindered and reduce entire districts to ashes.   The government failed where only one could fail, and it shows what happens when there is a mega-earthquake in the Los Angeles area.   Hundreds of thousands will die and millions will lose their possessions.   Because if a government in a fire and earthquake area cuts millions of dollars from protective facilities such as firefighters, ambulances and critical protective infrastructure, it should not be surprised if a disaster occurs, and for the potential victim it makes no difference which government is in power, whether Republican or Democrat.   Fires must be nipped in the bud.

Respectfully, the Santa Anas are nothing like a Cat 5 hurricane, whose *sustained* winds are 155mph. Never mind the speed of the gusts in a Cat 5. The Santa Anas are 40-60mph sustained winds with gusts of 70-100mph. The best analog would be a Tropical Storm which has roughly the same sustained wind and gust speeds. These are not hurricane force winds as the newscasters keep saying just because the maximum gust speed of the Santa Anas exceeds the minimum sustained wind speed for a Cat 1.  I lived in SoCal for 11 years and SE Louisiana for 12 years and I've experienced all of this up to a Cat 3 (in my youth ... I evacuated for Katrina). The winds of the Santa Anas are not comparable to a hurricane, even when ignoring the moisture content just as there's no comparison between a tropical storm and a Cat1 Hurricane. You ride the storm out, you leave for the hurricane.

Here in South Africa, local government typically disapprove wooden home. As a result, these are in a vast minority.  We typically build with baked bricks and mortar, and this does reduce fire risk.  In informal settlements, which aren't legal, there's no building code, and fire engines just can't get in to quench shack fires.  Often, when there's a fire, hundreds or even thousands lose everything, and they typically aren't ensured.  Calamitous. I prefer a proper masonry house. We also see that new roofs often get blown off during Cape storms, but centuries-old properly constructed roofs sit unscathed.

So much heartache for you all. So much lived experience here downunder.¬† It is terrifying. These fires are igniting in winter at low atmospheric temperatures. 30 something years ago the fires in our Adelaide Hills were determined to have resulted from high winds causing live power lines to arc. These were not downed powerlines. They were still secure on the poles. The resulting sparks start spot fires that spread. To stop power lines arcing you need to turn off the power. When you turn off the power you can not operate water pumps from¬†grid power, regardless of how much¬†water is available. As we see, once started fires in high winds continue to spot, are persistent and lethal.¬† But 30 years is a long time for the lessons learned in Adelaide to have sat on the shelf. Not to downplay the climate change imperative, please assess your response to this current emergency and the significant part the electricity delivery infrastructure has played. 'Build back¬†better' based on upgraded building codes and energy efficient underground power supply is theximperative, it is achievable. It offers a secure, insurable¬†future.¬† The ownership and energy your community brings to effecting a remedy is the stuff of¬† legend.¬†Take the electricity grid out of this fraction of the climate equation. ‚ù§

No matter how much coating or chemicals you use to make wood and paper walls fire resistant, it still is wood and paper. Plastic isn't any better. Use 'ash' instead. I mean things that are made of oxides, like Silicium Oxide and Aluminum Oxide. That's rock, glass, concrete and ceramics. These materials might crack but can't ever burn. They keep the heat of a fire on the outside for a long time. Metals are also good but different. They won't crack, but they are not good at keeping the heat outside. And as the video stated, some metals melt at a temperature low enough to fail in a wildfire. That's my opinion. I'm not an expert. I just know a little bit about chemistry.

The term ‚Äúunprecedented‚Äù means there is no precedent.  It means it has never happened before.  In this case there are plenty of precedents.  Nearly all forested and brush country west of the Mississippi has the potential for cataclysmic wild fires that in some cases can evolve into Dresden-like firestorms.   The Great Fire of 1910 for example burned 3 million acres.  Other fires of that era included reports of fire tornados.  The 1988 Yellowstone fires burnt over 1 million acres within the park and the adjacent lands.   People need to understand the dangers of building in fire prone areas.   Rule number one should be ‚Äúeven if it has not burnt in 100 years, it is still fire prone.‚Äù   The time since the last fire is only a measure of the amount of fuel that has accumulated.   Forests grow and develop over periods measuring 100‚Äôs of year; some trees  - such as Lodgepole Pine even require fire for their seeds to germinate.  Fire is built into the ecology and is an essential part of it.  Humans only think about the 5-10 years we will own the house.  Until we change how we build in these areas we will keep having these ‚Äúunprecedented‚Äù tragedies.

I'm from Australia as well and as Jon Anderson mentioned things are rated at varying BAL(Bushfire Attack Levels). The numbers in the rating represent kW/m2. And range through BAL Low, BAL 12.5, BAL 19, BAL 29, BAL 40 and BAL FZ(Flame Zone).   Pain to humans after 10 to 20 seconds - 4kW/m2 Pain to humans after 3 seconds - 10 kW/m2 Self ignition of cotton after a long time - 25kW/m2 Self ignition of timber after a long time - 25kWm2 Self ignition of cotton after 5 seconds - 42kW/m2 Self ignition of timber after 20 seconds - 45kW/m2   Fortunately my  house site is largely surrounded by cleared land ~100 m or 300+ ft.   I have just completed my Bushfire Management Statement which came out at BAL 12.5 which requires specific construction methods for dealing with embers such as materials that can be used, the need to use mesh etc.  The following discusses various building elements and their requirements https://ncc.abcb.gov.au/editions/2016/ncc-2016-volume-two/part-37-fire-safety/part-374-bushfire-areas.  The overall standards come under AS3959  Our new regulations largely came about from special investigations into the Black Saturday fires of 2009 https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Saturday_bushfires when 173 people died and over 2,000 homes were lost.  I nearly lost my brother, a firefighter, to a bushfire two weeks ago. Fortunately he is on the mend.  Last but by no means least my very best wishes to those who lost loved ones and their homes in the LA fires.

Another Australian to add some more comments that you totally missed The radiant heat alone from a crowing gum tree fire can set a house ablaze from over 100 yards away and pine burns hotter than gum so you need to protect from both radiant heat and from embers . The USA knows how to construct a self cleaning roof , most of the ones above the snow lines are self cleaning and snow is stickier than embers so simple lines self cleaning roofs are essential. By far the best fire proof roof construction is steel over furnace mill board because it will not burn no matter what, even if the heat & wind melts or buckles the steel & it blows away . Mill board under tiles or slates will also work really well but it does add a fair bit of weight so stronger trusses or closer trusses are needed  Guttering needs to be metal and have a leaf guard of some kind to prevent leaves building up in them . Gutters also need to have a plug in the downpipe or a valve in the downpipe so they can be filled with water thus any embers falling off the roof land it the gutter "moat "so more gutter hangers are needed . Eave lining has to be furnace mill board so a fire up a wall does not set fire to the eaves then enter the ceiling cavity . Every house within 5 miles of a forest must have a roof top sprinkler system that covers the roof + at least 10' around the house perimeter and the best material for around the perimeter is grass using water from a rain tank . Wet grass is unbelievably fire retarding and it catches embers so they don't blow around  and accumulate in a corner , particularly if it is cut 4" tall . Having a lot of rainwater tanks takes a lot of strain off local water storage and if they have fittings for fire hoses ( mandatory in Australia ) then the fire department will always have 5,000 gallons available to fight fire on each house without touching the hydrants . Glass is a massive problem as a lot of houses catch fire because radiant heat passing through the window ignites fine mesh see through curtains on the inside of the house . The only safe thing is fire screens or shutters not much different to storm shutters , even those roll down shutters reflect a massive amount of heat but they can warp so be impossible to open after a big fire . Lots of fire proof glass from borosilicate to double & triple laminated but yes it has to be in either a wood or metal frame , metal for brick or concrete walls & wood for wooden walls . The mesh you showed ( there are lot of them ) makes excellent fly screens for windows & doors which will also can also double as a fire mesh .  FWIW I moved into the NSW Blue Mountains in the 80's and we could not get fire insurance then that was affordable so after sitting on top of the roof for 2 weeks with a hose in my hand I saw the light & fitted 2 large rainwater tanks to the house then a roof top sprinkler that covered the roof and the yard from left fence to right fence . This not only made the house very fire safe but as we do not have stormwater pipes in this area it got rid of the swampy ground around the adsorption trench and I used up to 1/2 of each tank for garden use & latter on toilet flushing . In high summer with 110 F days I turned on these sprinklers to turn the whole roof into an evaporative cooling system . The gardens around the house were shifted away from the walls and most highly flammable plants removed .Succulents & cacti do not burn . Net result . Every neighbours house , the 3 behind + either side + the 3 over the road all suffered fire damage while ours did not and the cost was about 2 months wages using 2nd hand materials with me doing the labour . I did of course already have a diesel generator because we lost electrical power as a regular occurrence . I process building permits in the City of LA for a living and the areas that were burned down are considered  to be in Very High Fire Hazard Severity Zones  (VHFHSF). The VHFHSZ abide by their own set of regulations/ policies that help protect homes in those areas against fires or at least to slow them down. A requirement is that all of the building materials that are used be non-combustible.

The fires in LA have been incredibly tragic, with so many people losing their homes and, heartbreakingly, their lives. While we can‚Äôt change what has happened, we can think about how to create a better future.  I grew up in a small town in the Midwest and completely understand the appeal of single-family homes. But for the past 30 years, I‚Äôve lived in South Korea, where I‚Äôve seen an entirely different approach to urban development. Here, new cities are regularly built from the ground up, designed for efficiency, sustainability, and community.  One example is Gwanggyo Lake Park, where I now live. It‚Äôs a ‚Äúnew city‚Äù with around 30,000 apartments (condominiums, as Americans would call them). The design encourages walking, with everything you need‚Äîschools, shops, parks, and restaurants‚Äîjust a short walk away. Cars are hardly necessary.  This got me thinking: when was the last time the United States built an entirely new city? With all the space in the burned neighborhoods of LA, imagine the possibility of rebuilding in a way that could house hundreds of thousands of people efficiently‚Äîperhaps 300,000 apartments or more‚Äîwhile prioritizing safety, sustainability, and community.  It‚Äôs hard to capture in a comment how livable and pleasant these types of cities are, but I‚Äôm really curious: what do people think about this kind of rebuilding approach? Secondary factors in these forecasts of attacking the enemy were population density, topography and weather. The saved house was more likely saved due to the recent code adoption of 1-hour fire-rated eaves within 5 feet of a property line. Rather than creating a 1-hour eave, he removed the eaves on the property line side. That is why there are non-rated eaves in the front of the home, which he was not concerned with. This is minimum compliance to pass the plan check. Pure LUCK. It's a code requirement to keep the next-door fire not extending to your home. It is not the sign of a great architect. The fact is that luckily the fire did not hit the front of the property and the side of the property was protected with the current code model.

There are stringent building codes in CA for building in high fire hazard areas. Building products are required to be WUI complaint and meet Chapter 7a of the¬†CA Building code or it will not pass plan check or get passed by the building inspector. Mostly impacts the envelope of the building, such as, siding, roof, eaves, venting, etc. All buildings, in these zones, even residential are required to have fire sprinklers. Once the homes that burned are rebuilt, they will have to meet these updated building codes. Not easy keeping up with the building codes CA, as many of them vary by region, coastal, earthquake fault zones, high fire, snow, and on and on. Topography and weather varies so much across the state, which is what makes it so nice to live there.

In my city, we live east of the downtown area with prevailing west winds that funnel off the Columbia River Gorge.  In historical times, about a hundred years ago, the city burned to the ground because of widespread wood construction of commercial and residential buildings during that era. The city was rebuilt, but basically the same construction was used and is still in use in the residential/commercial/retail center of town. West of town is a vast flat valley of desert grasslands that, if they burn, would expose old town to fires beyond the local fire department's ability to control. Because of lack of funds as a retiree, I cannot afford to make my home completely fire safe, but I have basic firefighting equipment like a rated fire helmet with full visor, firefighting gloves and leather boots. But when push comes to shove, we are out the door and hustling down the street. The batteries are limited but the run time is 4X what it is said they would need to be to defend a passing fire.  He had put in large tanks  including an underground one for the fire pumps and has a Dam and a swimming pool.   The pumps are all contained in the pump house which connects the pool filtration and the fire pumps and allows the feeds to be interconnected.   He had other petrol Pumps feeding from these with hoses and sprinklers.  His idea was to not need any help to defend the place.  He was only there 2 Years when the big fires of 20-21 hit in OZ.  The Firey's came  and said the houses next door were indefensible due to the scrub  virtualy touching the properties  and they were the biggest threat to mates place if they went up.  They staged at his place ( 20 acre Lot) because they said they could easy defend his place and didn't even need an appliance.   The fire came, they waited then told him when to fire the pumps up and defended the place mainly from the houses next door when they went up.  I went there the next day and apart from the place looking a bit grubby from the ash, was completely untouched.  11 of the 14 Homes on his road were lost.   He said the price of defending the place with the equipment was nothing compared to what he would loose insurance or not and like me, cannot understand why people don't invest in ANYTHING to even have a chance of defending themselves.   While it would be unlikley to ever have another serious fire in the next 20 Years, he's since put in proper  4" Lines and hydrants around the place and a large decommissiond Diesel pump from a High rise building that's virtually  new that can feed from the underground tank and the dam he has made bigger.  He even ran a Line all the way to the front of the property so Fire trucks can fill from there.   There is a lot you can do to give yourself a better chance and any preparedness increases your chances exponentially over Doing nothing. The first summer he was there he had a couple of Petrol Pumps on agricultural Sprinklers which he could  start and then go which would have also hedged his chances of the place surviving a LOT.   I rate the chances of my own home ever being in a Fire about .0001% However I still have a portable petrol Pump I maintain, proper hoses and a tank that is always kept full except when I flush it when I know there is heavy rain coming.  I have a powerful electric pump that will run a proper 3/4" Fire hose and Nozzle with a battery pack  that is a backup power source for the house but also is never run down thanks to setting a Minimal discharge rate on the pack.   Sure, it's complete and utter overkill and the chances of it ever being needed are that of winning the lottery but when I see things like this, it's worth every cent 5X over for the comfort it gives me being able to sleep at night.

Us folks in rural CA understand fire hardening/preparedness as well as any.  As of 2019 there's been a law on the books that requires a defensible space inspection within first year after purchase of any home built before 2020, in a very high risk zone.  There is not sufficient fire dept staffing, especially in rural communities, to enforce this requirement.  5 feet of "defensible space" in a fire storm is meaningless.  CAL FIRE requires a min of 30' of non-vegetative boundary and another 70' of very controlled vegetation.  Sprinklers are clearly a low cost high value mitigation technique for fire at a distance, but, probably not in extreme wind conditions like this.  The 4' concrete boundary wall on that house didn't protect it.  This was a wind blown fire at a greater height.  I think the primary new takeway from the Palisades fire - (we already know quite a bit about rural wildfires) is that a)AHJs need to consider all climactic risks when determining development zones and b) they need to re-evaluate density requirements.  The high density development I see in many of these LA fires is just a continuous source of fuel for the fire.  We should not be building structures that densely.  There's also now a very tight relationship between insurance coverage (and cost) and fire hardening, but, the incentives have gone awry in my opinion.  I live in a rural setting with nearly 5000 gal of storage, a fire code compliant NST hydrant and roof sprinkler system and I get ZERO discount from the state fire hazard plan ("FAIR Plan") because I'm NOT hooked up to a public water system.  That's an example of stupid, anachronistic rules that need to evolve.  Here are the hardening criteria for discounts through the FAIR plan, which do make sense.      Class-A Fire rated roof (meaning composition shingle, stone, concrete or clay tile, ormetal)  ‚Ä¢6 inches at the bottom of all exterior walls made of non-combustible material ‚Ä¢Ember and fire-resistant vents (wire mesh covering) ‚Ä¢Upgraded windows (Multi-paned) ‚Ä¢Enclosed eaves Cleared vegetation and debris from under decks ‚Ä¢A 5 foot ember-resistant zone around the home (including fencing within 5 feet) ‚Ä¢Removal of combustible sheds and other outbuildings from the immediate surroundings of the home, to at least a distance of 30 feet ‚Ä¢Defensible space compliance (including trimming trees, removal of brush anddebris from yard, in compliance with California Public Resources Code 4291)

Several insurers have refused to offer fire policies because the risk is too high. That's because, for the past few years, insurance companies have been canceling policies or refusing to write new ones in California, citing rising risk of wildfires. As a result, the FAIR Plan's number of homeowner policies grew to more than 451,000 as of September 2024, an increase of 123% over the past three years. FAIR plans, also known as Fair Access to Insurance Requirements plans, are state-mandated property insurance plans that provide coverage to individuals and businesses who are unable to obtain insurance in the regular market.
Hi Matt, great video! While many builders and building scientists recommend having an unvented roof on their home for fire safety (no vents for embers to enter), the reality is that there are still millions of homes with a ‚Äútraditional‚Äù vented attic that can be retrofitted for fire safety. Therefore, in addition to having a class A fire rated roof covering (asphalt shingles, metal panels, clay tiles, etc) with a high temperature underlayment you can also install fire-wise attic eave vents - two companies that make them are Brandguard and Vulcan Vents. Another critical vent location is at the ridge, and Lumanco makes the LPR and VUR aluminum ridge vents (not plastic) that are better suited to handling high heat than plastic. All crawlspace vents should be "fire-wise" metal as well as vent outlets for range hood exhaust, dryer exhaust and for all other HVAC exhaust locations. All soffit and fascia material should be either metal covered (or fibre cement) and all metal gutters and downpipes. Lastly, I would use high temperature silicone ‚Äúboots‚Äù around plumbing vent pipes sticking up through the roof and then cover them with metal. One resource on fire-wise vents you could look at is called ‚ÄúThe Vulnerability of Vents to Wind Born Embers‚Äù (August 2017) by Stephen L. Quarles of the IBHS (available online in PDF).

Another OZ Engineer, the BAL ratings discussed by @jonanderson481 come from the Australian Standard AS-3959.  Within this code it sets out what you need to do to achieve different BAL ratings.  The level of the BAL rating you need to achieve is dependent on how far away you are from trees of different sizes (so the width of that 'defensible zone').  Items that are discussed are, minimum pitch of the roof, flat areas in front of windows (so slops on sills to allow embers to fall off), maximum holes sizes for vents (which is what that Stainless Mess is used for), minimum thickness and temper of glass.

It is possible to easily design a house that is TOTALLY fire-safe.  You build a house that, at the approach of a wildfire, can be shut down into a fire-proof box.  Not just a house with a metal roof but a house with metal siding and BIG SLIDING METAL BARN-DOORS that cover up any and all windows or porches or any other avenue by which fire might enter the house.  It's that simple.  Shut down your house and leave.  Return, slide the big metal barn-doors back, and your house is just as it was.  In the meantime, fire crews don't have to waste resources protecting your house.  This fire-proof box design should be in the building code, state-wide, for any house built in extreme wildfire-hazard areas.        Metal roof/siding/barn-doors are not that expensive for a small house.  If the building code requires newly-built houses be fire-safe boxes, the firefighters would be able to focus on defending houses built before this provision in the code.  This provision in the code should have existed YEARS ago.  it is, it is outrageous that the ultra-rich built $20,000,000 houses and did not use those millions to build a fire-safe house.  The press should grill them about their irresponsible folly.         The builders and developers should have no problem with this provision in the code.  Houses will still be built.  The builders and developers will simply be building fire-safe houses, probably at a greater profit to themselves.

Another takeaway from this heartbreaking, catastrophic fire is the role that water pressure loss in the neighborhood water systems deprived firefighters the water they need to fight the fire.  What I never considered was that each house that burns has plastic service piping or supply lines which melt and immediately begin leaking  up to 40-50 gallons per minute. Multiply this by 4-5 homes (let alone a whole neighborhood) and it is no wonder why there is no water pressure.  While it is not reasonable that fleeing home owners will turn their water off at the street, there should be heat activated valves that will shut off water services in the case of fire.

Good info, and timely.  The greater LA area has special issues: steep hills and canyons where ground based firefighters can get badly trapped and it takes air assets to fight a blaze. Of note, eaves trap heat and become channels next to a brush area.  Older 20's/30's homes used a lot of so called "gingerbread" in these areas - that is a mistake in these fire zones, as they can trap embers and... up it goes.   As for chimoneas... an utterly stupid device that will consume excessive wood for any warmth given off - and they mostly don't come with a stack screen: make your own.  If I had one, bottom gets a foam liner and the cat can sleep in it.  A general tip on combustion risks [other than charcoal bbq]  is to keep them 10 feet or better from a house.  I am always gobsmacked when I see emergency generators 5 feet from a house because offset from the eave edge is 2 feet looking up - God help you if that throws a rod while running. So the question is: what is the best material/construction to close in eaves, assuming fire safety type vent screens?  The Paradise fire jolted a fresh interest in specialized  vents years ago - they may not block heat, but  certainly embers and major ash.

Australian here. We have all our rain gutters covered in the fine stainless steel mesh shown on this video. The mesh runs from the metal roof sheet over the gutter. It excludes leaves and embers. We have many other fire measures such as surroundind hard stand and on site water, but the mesh would be easy for most people to install.
Where I live in the midwest most new wood frame homes today are built with 2x4's or similar, plywood, a moisture barrier, vinyl siding and windows. They go up quick in a fire, get blown down into little pieces in a tornado. We did walk throughs in the fire service of new buildings going up. We knew right away there was little thought given to fire prevention in residential homes. Many homes I have seen in the south are still made with real brick masonry. These do far better in tornadoes and external fires. I wouldn't buy a standard wood frame house where I live.

In answer to a common question, the reason a lot of LA houses were historically built from wood is because it's an earthquake zone. Like in Japan, the wood flexes, absorbing the horizontal vibrations, rather than collapsing. Newer buildings are built on bases that can move with the primary shear line (base isolation), or have methods of damping the movement. https://blog.ringfeder.com/compelling-reasons-for-earthquake-resistant-construction

Some context for the Europeans in the comment section. Look up a global seismic hazard map and historical population growth. Northern Europe does not get real earthquakes. Italy's earthquakes are about as bad as coastal Oregon. Southern Spain's risk is similar to Vancouver, Canada. The closest place to Europe with a similar seismic risk as LA is Eastern Turkey near the Iranian border. Don‚Äôt forget population growth. Somewhere like France has only doubled their population since 1860, whereas California has a hundred times their 1860 population. Combine such growth with restrictive single use zoning laws since the 1910s, overpriced land from prop 13 since the 1970s and US mortgage subsidies since the 1930s and you're left with large expensive lots you can only build a single home on, leaving less of the budget for the actual building.

In South Africa 99% of houses and buildings are built out of brick or concrete, and we have either clay tiles, cement tiles or corrugated iron roofing which doesn't burn when embers land on it. Every year as fire season approaches, the local authorities burn a 300 metre wide firebreak around the entire town, doing it on calm days. The farmers also burn 50 to 100 metre wide firebreaks around their fields. All tree plantations trim all the lower branches to a height of 10 metres and clear away these branches which are burnt on a calm day. There is a 50 metre wide corridor under all main power lines where all growth is removed to prevent fires from sparks and also damge to the lines when fire occurs. And there's much more. I can't think of any place in SA that has burnt down except a large upmarket thatched complex in the Cape that was caught in a bush fire about 10 years ago because embers landed on the thatched roofs - dumb design.

I designed a fireproof home for a coworker in Coal Creak Canyon outside Denver.  Steel roof, steel studs and joists. Stucco outside walls and an outside fire suppression system with a 4,000 gal cistern with a non electric pump to make sure it would work if the electrical system failed and a gravel surround barrier around the outside of the house.  The owner commented that he was not sure he would want to live there if everything around him burned. I'm surprised he didn't mention hempcrete. Hempcrete is a more environmentally friendly alternative to concrete that uses waste material from hemp plants. It is lighter and easier to work with than concrete and is extremely fire resistant. Also, unlike concrete, it is carbon negative, hempcrete actually absorbs carbon dioxide throughout its life span and is mold and rot resistant. Several companies make them as easy to assemble blocks  that can replace mason blocks or they can be used with regular stick framing. Hempcrete blocks act as their own vapor barrier and insulation with an R-value that's on par with traditional 2x4 walls with fiberglass, so they can simplify home building by eliminating the need for insulation and vapor barriers. Sativa Building Systems "Z-Panel" was one option I was exploring for my future home, once they make it to full scale production. It has a Class A fire rating.

I live in central WA state and have been thru 5 range fires, three of which burned right up to my defenses. When I built, I received a pamphlet from the permitting office that explained how to layout my landscaping for fire resistance. I was in a saddle above a steep slope covered in short sagebrush and other shrub steppe wild things. The pamphlet showed how far flames being blown up that slope would go at various wind speed. The max shown was 200 ft. Darn it, my house was not 200 ft away from my property line. I can and do thin all the way down that slope to the property line. I thin because clearing requires something to hold soil and I am not rich enough for that. During the first fire, which was caused by teenagers tossing firecrackers out their car window, I learned that the winds coming down my narrow valley wrap around a bowl in the terrain and speed up as they are concentrated up and over my saddle (where the house is). The heat from that once scorched a small mugo pine that is about 15 ft from my house. The fire had creeped into short-mowed dry grass and, even though stopped by a gravel road and 10 ft of pea gravel landscaping, had scorched the tree. I was lucky it did not ignite (I keep it well-watered). The pamphlet also required all my trees and tall shrubs be free of foliage to the 6 ft mark. I made sure of that and the deer keep them cleaned off to just about that anyway. I ran a circle drive of gravel around my house. I mow on the other side of that. Gravel from the drive to the buildings, with some plants interspersed in the 5-30 ft defense line. No plants in the 5‚Äô zone next to buildings. All my buildings are metal sided and roofed but a fireman told me radiant heat will ignite wood behind it. That is why the fire barrier behind metal was mentioned in this video. I do not have to deal with many embers because the nearest forest is not in the wind path to my house, but I have every opening in my buildings sealed with the 1/8‚Äù steel mesh anyway. I keep the nooks and crannies around my buildings clear of leaves and debris. When a fire is headed my way I have a fire cart with all my important things to be loaded in my Honda Element along with my dogs and old cat and I can leave in 10 minutes; I have arranged with friends to bunk with them until I can get back home. I do not worry about my buildings because I compete with only 3 other properties during fire triage. They protect mine first mainly because it takes the brunt of the fire and if they can stop it at my place then the others are less likely to ignite. However, firefighters take one look at my place and move on quite quickly to the next easiest to save. Those who have not bothered to fireproof their properties are last; it is efficient. They cannot spend the time it would take to save 3 houses  on trying to save one house. It‚Äôs triage.  My fire cart holds a portable file box for the important papers, thumb drives of photos, carry-on luggage with clothing and toiletries, and my portable fire-resistant safe with the REALLY important papers, and the cat carrier. Leashes are always in the car. And I run as soon as I can figure out that I am in the path of the fire. My road out will be choked with speeding vehicles driven by terrified people INTO the fire area to help their friends and family get livestock out. There are companies who are contracted to bring semis in to get livestock, all on a two lane with crumbling shoulders. You want to get out before these people head in. They take up the entire road on every corner. I advise not to build a house in a saddle, on a point blocking off a valley down which 45 and 55 mph winds will drive a fire. Do use metal exterior but be sure to have a fire block of some kind behind that metal. Build fire-wise. Good luck to all.

I am not a builder, but I am a certified landscaper.  California has a 2 fold issue: first major earthquakes, second fire!  Both must be considered together!  Concrete, cinder block or brick won't do well in a quake, wood or vinyl siding and roofing is a fire problem.  I would prefer steel, concrete board, or stucco siding over a bias strapped corners wood frame.  From British tower fires aluminum poly clad and Aluminum composite materials (ACM) can be a risk. as the poly membrane often can burn  Landscaping: no plants close or adjacent to the house, especially no woody plantings. Gravel or river rock along the house, or concrete slabs I used dark blue granite gravel over a French drain in front of my 1950's home in a re-sodding, foundation insulating and drainage project last summer!  I live in the soggy North East so I had a wet basement, even though we have had several 3 to 6inch events since, I have had no water in the house.  Away from me.  In Chilli, they are using mesh screens (similar to the rollup mesh tarps on dump trucks) to extract water from cooling air high on mountains, could California use that to keep vegetation greener in higher elevations by drip irrigating the landscape high up in the hills and mountains.  I am not saying that - that would stop fires, but everything matters in slowing fire enough that we can stop it!  Water Supply:  1. Empty water supply. 2. Power turned off in fear of power grid being responsible or able to create more fire sources! 3. I am OK with turning off power, but municipalities and grid owners MUST provide emergency power for pumping stations that can provide 96 hours of PEAK emergency operation.  Solar, with Wind (BOTH) and a battery storage like a Tesla Power Wall.  Why Solar with Wind, fires are not a biggy with no wind, and the two together offers redundancy plus aren't generators banned in 2027 or something?  Even without the reservoir full, the pumps being powered down in my opinion without backup power is far worse than that empty reservoir!  Also single windmills transmit less harmonic waves to the properties where as multiple windmills can have audible harmonics that can even loud at a miles distance!   If a windmill is PURE mechanical energy turning a pump that can be far more efficient compared to a windmill making electricity then using the electricity to power a motor turning a pump. As a bonus maybe the system can permanently sever the grid connection to the pumps saving power everyday!  Remember we need that power to power all electric vehicles and this would be an easy way to save some. As 30yr Firefighter, Fire Inspector and Fire Investigator as well as Wildland Firefighting certified I have just a few comments. (Sorry for Credentialing)First, yes this is an unbelievable tragedy and I feel for the victims. I was going to elaborate about construction materials and techniques but I think we got that. But I'll just say, it's not just about your house. It's a neighborhood. If the house next to you doesn't burn as rapidly then there is less fire to get to your home and so on. Lastly, the surroundings. We've heard alot about water supply and yes that's hugely important. But the biggest issue was surrounding terrain. No attempt to mitigate uphill fire extension with any form of firebreaks. Terraced hillsides to slow down water runoff and keep vegetation greener and moist, which slows fires moving uphill. Many small water capture dams and ponds for the same thing. I'm sorry, but complaining about drought when most water is just allowed to runoff to the ocean is counterproductive. Two years ago, the area got record rains, talked about atmospheric rivers, to the point of flooding and making the news. But, all that water was just allowed to runoff to the ocean. Capture some it, slow it down so things stay greener, and allow it to soak into the soil. Everything that slows the fire, helps prevent its spread. Good luck in the future.

What most ppl seem to miss is, in usual home construction, the attic has soffit vents and roof vents to keep hot air from building up in the attic and to also prevent moisture build up. That vent combo creates at CHIMNEY effect in the attic. So during a wildfire event, blowing sparks get sucked into attics by that chimney effect and catch them on fire. You'd think by now it'd be in the building code to have all those homes equipped with soffit and roof vent shutters that could be closed during times you want to STOP the chimney effect.  Notice how Tom Hanks house has no attic, no soffit or roof vents. No way for flying sparks to get inside and start a fire inside the home.

South eastern Australia has lots of information about building in high risk bushfire zones. Our house survived a bushfire in February 2024. We had been preparing our timber house for bushfire for over 20 years. Defendable space, roof sprinkler system, independent water supply, fuel pump so we were not reliant on electricity, and metal-meshing all gaps. Well maintained garden and surrounds all help. Careful garden plant selection and placement. We were there to defend with support of fire fighters.

Small correction on your comparison. 96-110 mph sustained winds are a category 2 hurricane. Category 5 is equal to or greater than 157 mph sustained winds. The damaged level difference between a Cat 2 or Cat 5 hurricane is extensive. In a Cat 2 there will be extensive damage to structures but they will stay standing. Very few structures can survive a Cat 5. Hurricane Katrina made landfall as a Cat 3. The day before it was a Cat 5 with 175 mph winds.

As usual a very thoughtful presentation.  A couple of notes.  CA building code instituted in 2008 mandates nearly all of these construction techniques in wildfire prone areas however it is up to the local goverments to adopt and enforce them.  It is my understanding that Palisades has adopted this code and Greg's house would have been required to incorporate nearly all the things he'd did.    I am a Northern California builder and virtually every project incorporates these measures in the areas I work in,  Class A roofs, fireproof soffits and eaves, intumescent vents or ventless construction, tempered glass etc. ¬†Some cities are requiring a 5' wide noncombustible perimeter. An interesting note, metal roofs are not Class A.  They need an approved underlayment.  Also Ipe' wood decks are Class A.  There is little published data on  the performance of these hardened structures vs their neighbors,  One study claimed that hardened structures had double the survival rate as trad structures.  However they were still only 40% likely to survive.  As Greg noted they were very lucky.  I've been directly involved in five wild fires over the years.  I now live in downtown Napa and in the 2017 Tubbs fire sent an ember as big as your head into my yard from 5 miles away.  When  that north east wind blows hard nothing is safe.

Their population went up by 60% the past 40 years, but no effort to protect the people. Criminal on all counts. The US started building out of wood early in its growth and settlement by Europeans. It was cheap and plentiful resource. As a result more industrial production surrounded it, tradesman got it to perfection in building with it, accessories were developed around it, and the American culture sees a wood home as ‚Ä¶ a home. Warm inviting etc. the challenge is that building with other materials; brick, stone, steel, composites, rammed earth, etc etc is not common or culturally desirable. However, if you‚Äôre building in a wildfire zone (or any unique climate) it should be considered what the most appropriate materials would be to minimize risk . Risk of fire, flood, ice, mudslides, tornado, earthquakes, etc. You wouldn‚Äôt build a house in the ocean front without building it on piers, why build with wood when it‚Äôs one of the most combustable materials . Hopefully when they rebuild they consider this fact.

The 2018 Camp Fire in Paradise, California, showed that even homes built to modern fire-resistant standards were often destroyed when surrounded by intense, fast-moving flames. In such conditions, mitigation efforts failed to prevent widespread destruction.   A study from the Insurance Institute for Business & Home Safety (IBHS) found that while defensible space reduced losses in low- to moderate-intensity fires, it was largely ineffective in high-intensity events.  That homeowner was lucky, as the fire that hit near his home likely wasn't severe (all the trees still intact on the far right). While the defensible space and fire-resistant materials helped, it's not fool proof. In fires like the Palisades Fire, where environmental factors (e.g., high winds, extreme drought, and heat) create uncontrollable conditions, even the best mitigation efforts are unlikely to prevent large-scale destruction. These fires highlight the limitations of individual and community-level measures when faced with extreme regional climate-driven events. Also these mitigation methods are costly and not everyone can afford them, making the current housing situation far worse.

Concrete and bricks are generally not preferred for construction in earthquake zones due to several key factors:  ## Lack of Ductility  One of the main reasons concrete and bricks are not ideal for earthquake-prone areas is their lack of ductility. Ductility refers to a material's ability to deform without failure[2][5]. Unlike materials such as steel, wood, or bamboo, concrete and bricks are brittle materials that cannot absorb the energy from an earthquake effectively[1]. When subjected to the back-and-forth motion of seismic waves, these materials are more likely to crack, crumble, or collapse suddenly.  ## Poor Performance Under Seismic Loads  Unreinforced masonry buildings, including those made of bricks, concrete blocks, or adobe, perform very poorly during earthquakes[1][9]. The mortar holding the masonry together is generally not strong enough to resist earthquake forces. This can lead to walls falling away or buckling, resulting in significant damage or complete collapse of the structure.  ## Vulnerability to Shaking  During an earthquake, the shaking causes buildings to move in a horizontal direction, which can be disastrous for structures primarily designed to support vertical loads[2]. Concrete and brick buildings, especially if unreinforced, are particularly vulnerable to this lateral movement. The rigid nature of these materials means they cannot flex and sway with the ground motion, leading to increased stress on the structure.  ## Risk of Falling Debris  In the event of an earthquake, unreinforced concrete and brick buildings pose a significant danger due to falling debris[1]. Parapets, chimneys, cornices, and other ornamental features made of these materials can easily break away from the building, creating a hazard for people both inside and outside the structure.  ## Alternative Materials and Techniques  Instead of using concrete and bricks alone, modern earthquake-resistant construction often employs:  1. Reinforced concrete: When properly designed and constructed with steel reinforcement, concrete can be made more ductile and earthquake-resistant[8][10].  2. Wood and steel: These materials have more flexibility and are favored in earthquake zones[12].  3. Base isolation systems: These systems use flexible pads to separate a building's foundation from the ground, allowing it to move independently during an earthquake[2].  4. Damping technologies: Shock absorbers and other damping devices can help dissipate seismic energy[2].  While concrete and bricks can still be used in earthquake-prone areas, they require special design considerations, reinforcement techniques, and strict adherence to building codes to ensure safety. In many cases, other materials and construction methods are preferred for their superior performance under seismic loads.  Citations: [1] https://www.portland.gov/ppd/unreinforced-masonry-urm-buildings [2] https://planhub.com/resources/how-earthquake-proof-buildings-are-built/ [3] https://www.reddit.com/r/StructuralEngineering/comments/kwty07/reinforced_concrete_or_wood_what_material_is/ [4] https://en.poyatos.com/blog/the-use-of-concrete-in-seismic-zones [5] https://blog.iseekplant.com.au/blog/5-features-earthquake-proof-building [6] https://acropolis-wp-content-uploads.s3.us-west-1.amazonaws.com/2019/02/reinforce-v2.gif?sa=X&ved=2ahUKEwiji4S9pPCKAxWcRTABHUv0C4MQ_B16BAgDEAI [7] https://www.americanprecastfences.com/is-precast-concrete-earthquake-resistant/ [8] https://www.foxblocks.com/blog/earthquake-resistant-construction-techniques [9] https://www.earthquakecountry.org/step4/urmwalls/ [10] https://www.britannica.com/technology/earthquake-resistant-construction [11] https://www.latimes.com/local/lanow/la-me-ln-how-l-a-conquered-an-earthquake-danger-zone-brick-buildings-20151009-story.html [12] https://www.exploratorium.edu/explore/seismic-science/engineering [13] https://www.preventionweb.net/files/7632_Earthquakesafeconstructionofmasonrybuildings.pdf [14] https://www.scienceworld.ca/resource/building-and-testing-earthquake-proof-structures/ [15] https://www.reddit.com/r/AskEngineers/comments/1cmzqyp/how_safe_are_brick_walls_in_an_earthquake_prone/ [16] https://www.bigrentz.com/blog/earthquake-proof-buildings

I've a 40 year construction background. Mostly in the tinderbox of the So. Cal forested areas. Now I'm in Montana. We've been using both tempered glass and non tempered wildland fire rated glass (not required in my area). The tempered works, but not as well as the treated fire rated regular glass. The fire rating is from the extra reflective coating of a special Low E coating. This combined with non opening wood framed glass, timber around that and a stone veneer toward the prevailing spark storm helps. The glass is only a part of a system when preparing for wildland fires.

There was a lot working against everyone in this tragedy. In many cases, the BEST anyone could do is barely create the 5 foot defensive space, because the homes are packed together so tightly; The vast majority of it came down to architecture. A lot of homes definitely had non native plants that are not fire/drought tolerant. Unfortunately, one misstep in design choices be it architectural, landscape and exterior furniture etc... can cost you dearly. I also think there's a lot of political misconceptions about why it keeps happening. Fire is part of nature. Natural fire is how the earth has managed land since its inception, but as urban sprawl happened strategies changed from set a perimeter and let it burn to we have to fight it to save property and now it's we have to fight it to save lives. For an area that averages 20 inches of rain annually, prescribed burns are kinda difficult to safely do. The terrain makes it difficult to 'manage' anything to begin, what's more fire creates its own weather (wind) that exacerbates the Santa Anas that were also an issue. Fire is required for some types of plants/trees out there to be able to germinate new ones with the seeds on the ground. Hopefully those who rebuild choose/can afford to do so and take the necessary steps to prevent their loss from happening again.

One feature about the fires in the LA area, Lahaina and Paradise and a few other places is they weren't like normal wildfires. While houses burned to the ground and engine blocks and glass in cars melted and ran down the pavement or street, surprisingly plastic trash and recycle bins did not melt even if they were right by the houses. In Lahaina where businesses also burned down, steel frame construction suffered the same fate as houses and steel beams that burn at higher temperatures than forest fires melted but the plastic bins sitting just feet away were not melted.    Also trees and scrubs didn't burn the same way they do in typical forest fires. Something very different was going on in the LA area and in Lahaina and in Paradise and needs to be investigated. Over the last 20 years California has essentially stopped all forestry on public lands.We are down to just a couple of sawmills now and the only time I see a lot of logs going into the sawmill near my house (Sonora California near Yosemite) is burned logs from one of the out of control fires that we have.The state has even made it difficult to do fire hardening by banning gas chainsaws and weed eaters. I went to Austria several years ago to look at their building techniques that are mostly block and decks or exterior exposed wood are large timber.What I was amazed by was how beautifully manicured the forest was driving through the countryside.You would even see random stacks of firewood between 2 trees perfectly stacked.Fire hardening and forest management will help but with 100 mph winds there is really nothing you can do but get out of the way.Often the contents of the house (like drapes and furniture)will catch on fire and tempered windows can help.One of the products that I was amazed by for exterior walls is called Durisol. It is a pre formed insulated block made out of mineralized recycled wood chips and I have built several houses with it in California. It is a little more expensive than wood frame but it will not burn,I even tried to light some on fire with an acetylene cutting torch one time.What really made the cost difference is all the shear panels and Simpson hardware we have to use for earthquakes and the labor to install all these things. My prayers are with everyone affected by the fires.

I am a retired wild land firefighter and live in a high threat fire area. We had the largest Arizona wild land fire that destroyed many homes . Before we evacuated I took my lawn tractor and took the yard down to bare earth all around my house. I had removed some trees and brought the outside area up to fire-wise specs. I watched the fire come down the mountain ( I was now working the fire with the fire dept. which I was a volunteer with) with 60-70 miles an hour speed. My neighbors cabin burned to the ground, the cabin across the road burned, a lot of other structures burned. The fire came right to the property line I had cleared to however we had a small planter  behind the cabin that had bark on it that acted like a match and the fire burned through the bark. The fire was so hot that the steel roof was blistered and the underlayer was cooked. I feel that clearing most  all the fuels on the ground helped a lot. The key is to prepare way before the threat is there.  I don't think there is a way to totally protect against a fire but looking at this video and common sense will help a lot.

I live in a fire prone area in australia  (surrounded by national parks) and have needed to evacuate 4 times over the years. The first time, the fire front was heading towards us in three directions. Twice the town was saved at the last minute due to change in wind direction. We soon learnt how to get "fire ready" and we do get reminded regularly. Sprinklers on the roof, blocking the downpipes with old clothes and filling the gutters with water. I now have a similar metal mesh over the gutter to prevent embers getting in the roof cavity. You may feel complacent if the fire is not close, but it needs to be taken seriously because of the ember attack. We now have 3 fire stations in my town, The normal fire and rescue, station, the bushfire unit (mainly volunteers) and the command centre that coordinates fire fighting etc. After the first major fire they would not allow any new housing estates to be established in my town and to my surprise they have adhered to it.  Each fire situation is different and to be honest I don't think the firefighters would have been able to do much in what happened in California due to the winds and age of the homes. The weather was the fires friend and the firefighters enemy. Hopefully the appropriate authorities will seriously review what happened and take steps, including updated building standards to prevent a similar thing from happening again. America its time to put aside political motives, bias and divide and tackle this ongoing threat as a country from the top down. No doubt that I will get negative comments but I did reside in the affected for a while.

We used to live in Colorado which is another state prone to wildfires.   We lived in a very small town with it's own fire dept.   The fire dept would come around once a year to inspect each property to see if each home owner did fire prevention for their property.   We had built our home, stucco outside, and a 5 foot perimeter of rock around the house.   No bushes.  We did have 2 trees in the back yard but we kept the lower tree limbs trimmed up pretty far.  I don't remember what the requirement was for how high to keep the limbs trimmed, but it met the fire detardent standards.    But if a home owner did not do their own fire prevention on their property everyone was warned by the fire dept that they would not try to save your house if a wild fire erupted.   The fire dept would only try to save homes whose owners took precautions.

I live near the Superior National Forest, we have had some big fires, but, no loss of life, however quite a bit of damage to property. We have a county wide FIRE WISE program that helps landowners mitigate the dangers of fire. One program that was funded in part by the Federal Government, helped homeowners install metal roofs, and, sprinkler systems. I had friends whose house survived while the ones on either side of their property burned. They had a sprinkler system, metal roofs, and, had cleaned up brush and debris and other flamable things from around their home and garage.

In UK we have Regulations about Unprotected Areas that define how much unprotected area is allowed depending on the distance from the boundary to limit flash over.  With the number of windows in the side wall I would suspect that the house shown would not pass UK Regs.  Also, metal roofs are not in themselves fire resistant.  The fact that the car is all metal didn't save it, its what you do behind the metal that is important.  Fireproof glazing can be had with Pyran glass that is laminated and has a fireproof core that holds the glass intact but like most glass its not good at preventing radiant heat igniting the other side of the glass.  Normally, the frame for Pyran would be timber or occasionally steel but not aluminium as that melts at relatively low temperatures.   Many UK building fire regs have their origins in the Great Fire of London 1666 when closely packed timber buildings burnt uncontrollably.  The rebuilding saw a great deal more emphasis placed on fire protection such that by 1940's when British cities were rained on with high explosives and incendiaries there was no widespread fire propagation.  Contrast that with Dresden when the whole city centre was burnt to the ground because they had retained their wooden buildings.

It was another wind fueled fire in Ventura, Ca powered by very strong winds that were estimated to be 60 to 70 mph. Fire started out in Santa Paula and went west due to the Santa Ana winds. It burned down a number of homes near my families home. We were fortunate as it didn‚Äôt reach. However, one of the things that was discovered after that fire is that those homes were built in the 70s and they have four holes drilled into the eaves all over for attic ventilation that are completely open but covered with quarter inch chicken wire to prevent embers from getting in the attic. However, what was discovered was a number of the homes caught fire because the embers would get in those eaves through the holes and light the attic on easily on fire due to dust, dry wood and other things people store in attics. Once the attic was on fire the home was a complete loss. As it‚Äôs almost impossible for the fireman to fight the fire because the roof is still intact.
