THIS TREE SHOULD BE BANNED IN CALIFORNIA. A eucalyptus tree can become sticky when burned because of its naturally high sap content, which releases a sticky resinous substance when the wood is heated during combustion; this is especially noticeable when burning eucalyptus leaves or small branches, making the flames and surrounding area potentially sticky. A burning ember from this tree can stick to the outside structure of buildings.  The eucalyptus sap is considered sticky and flammable due to its high content of volatile oils, making eucalyptus trees highly combustible; when heated, the sap releases flammable vapors that can easily ignite, contributing to the rapid spread of wildfires.

You stock the water in a large reservoir underground at one end of the garden and in case of fire, the water is transferred to the sprinklers by a battery-powered water pump. The sprinklers also work in short intervals, wetting the building and acting as a shield against incoming embers. Although it sounds a bit ridiculous, it can save your house when the fire department is useless. Since the amount of water the sprinkler will use is small, it can save you for a while at least.

The moment you mentioned that you didn't know what a 1-hour rated firewall is, it was surprising. As a European civil engineer, different types of firewalls and fire requirements are among the most important and closely scrutinized aspects. For example, in general, we cannot build flammable structures near other buildings. They must be at least 10 meters apart. If you want them to be closer, they need to be fireproof or separated by a concrete/brick fireproof wall that extends higher than the roofline.

Matt - There are a few videos out about some other people in the la fire scar area that also survived. They have the common characteristic of sprinkling their homes in the midst of the firestorm. One did it passively by setting up oscillating sprinklers on his roof and turning them on just before he left. The other had a gas powered pump attached to their pool with outlet hoses that the son father and grandfather took around the property to spray any embers that came near their house or their neighbors. Hebel Panel which is 50mm to 75mm thick is a great exterior vertical fitted panel that can be rendered to finish. Easy to cut for apertures. I think it was originally from Germany but is also now manufactured in Australia under license. We also use it in New Zealand and is fixed to a timber framed home.  Hebel systems have a Fire Resistance Level (FRL) of 60‚Äì240 minutes. This means they can withstand fire for 60‚Äì240 minutes. Hebel systems are also designed to meet or exceed the requirements for all six Bushfire Attack Level (BAL) categories.

In Australia we have had specific building regulations for construction in a fire prone region, based on the level of risk. We built a house 20yrs ago that backed on to a bush land reserve, we had to have metal screens, special glass in the windows, fire retardant paint on the exterior, sheet metal (Colorbond) roofing, minimum sized water tank on site with a fire hose fitting, and steel house frame‚Ä¶.there were probably other things I can‚Äôt remember, but it was all to help mitigate the risk of losing the home to fire caused by ember-strike.  It blows my mind that houses in LA are not subject to the same kind of standards, when wildfires are an annual occurrence, as they are here in many regions of Australia. South Australian here, in firestorms such as these, which are just about indefensible,  you need metal shutters, gutter guards to hinder ember ingress, water in gutters , metal roof, pebble dash or non flammable render on walls ,metal windows , water tanks , sprinkler systems to wet the building , generator to power water pumps  and about 20 feet without sizable vegetation near the home.Or you can build into a hillside ( not facing in the direction of prevailing winds )  and just have one wall open to the elements and then , use heavy duty shutters to cover the windows. None of this comes cheap but the movie stars could easily have built to this standard. I believe that the material used for a building's exterior is not as crucial for fire protection in urban areas as one might think. I am from Germany, where many houses are built exclusively from stone. There are numerous regulations prohibiting the use of highly flammable materials in construction.    A few years ago, a single-family home on my street burned down. The building's exterior consisted of two layers of brick, the window frames were made of aluminum, and the floors near the fire's origin were made of stone. The exterior windows were closed, and wood was burning in a glass-enclosed fireplace inside the house.    Alcohol was involved, and when more wood was added to the fire, the carpet caught fire. The entire house burned down and had to be demolished. Even the reinforced concrete ceiling was no longer stable. All the materials inside the house burned too easily, and the resulting heat compromised the building's integrity. Open floor plans further exacerbate the problem by allowing heat to spread more effectively.    In my opinion, for urban fire scenarios, a building exterior capable of withstanding the flames of a burning neighboring house or a car fire in front of the door for at least one hour is sufficient. By then, the fire department would arrive and prioritize protecting adjacent buildings. Such protection can even be achieved with a wooden wall insulated with sheep‚Äôs wool. What matters is that the exterior and interior layers have high density, giving the wall significant weight. The fire would need to heat a lot of material before the exterior becomes flammable.    In some wooden houses, the exterior wood layer was deliberately charred as a natural fire protection method. This practice, used as early as Viking times, also reliably protected the wood from moisture. Some wooden houses have stood for several hundred years.    I find the interior furnishings of houses to be highly relevant. Unfortunately, fewer solid, massive furniture pieces are being made today. Instead, more materials are used that burn quickly and easily, allowing fires to spread rapidly. While technical devices are designed to avoid causing fires, little attention is paid to ensuring that, should a fire occur, it remains localized.    Thoughts about protecting a house from fire should start here‚Äîwith practical tips like garden design, for example. In many cases, such measures would significantly reduce risk relative to the capital invested, more so than using stone, steel, or concrete. These materials can also become unstable due to the heat from an interior fire, and they may accumulate toxins that cannot always be fully removed for safe reoccupation.

I am constantly surprised by the fragile house builds that are so pervasive in the United States of America. Every time there is a natural disaster (tornado, hurricane, flood, fire) the news pictures of the aftermath give the impression of toothpicks, matchsticks and kindling as the primary construction materials. And seeing building contractor videos of one story houses going from foundation slab to dried in with roofing and siding in one week to less than a month (for a two story house) seems like pure insanity. I get the impression that houses are built to last only 15-20 years. (This is my perspective looking in from the outside.)  I have lived for almost five decades in South America. There the primary building material is [ceramic] bricks and concrete for the walls. The roof used to be wood framing with tiles. Newer houses used corrugated metal over the wood framing (rafters and perlings). In recent years the roof framing has switched to metal truss construction of various kinds, with sheet metal covering. For multistory houses the intermediate floors are almost always a concrete slab. And some times even the top ceiling is a concrete slab, usually covered by an inclined metal roof. The incidence of house fires is way below that seen in the USA, because the main structure is non-combustible. Also, by legal binding regulation, houses can only be depreciated (for tax purposes) in 25 years.  Even in Germany most houses (even single dwelling houses) are constructed with the outer shell of bricks, cement blocks and/or concrete. Though the interior non-load-bearing wall can be of wood framing covered with sheet-rock type material. Even the historical half-timbered houses have the major portion of their walls made from bricks, adobe or clay. It is not uncommon for houses to have been built well over 100 years ago. I have seen big buildings from the 1860s to the 1890s that are still part of the urban landscape. The reconstruction after World War 2 was facilitated by the fact that the outer shell had remained intact, so that only the burnt out interior and roof had to be reconstructed. The majority of current renovations consist of gutting and rebuilding the electrical, plumbing and heating installations, as well as replacing the outer doors and windows with more energy efficient variants. Mainly for houses that were constructed or last renovated more than 40-50 years ago.

The window glass? Many years ago I was designing fire resistant partitioning systems.  We used laminated intumescent glass. The standard we were aiming for 30 minutes with w fire which started at 850 centigrade and ramped up to 950 at 30 minutes.  The glass quickly became opaque and developed cracks, but was still intact at 38 minutes when the system finally failed.

It‚Äôs the roots of mushrooms, can be grown into molds, baked, made into thin sheets or made into bricks. It‚Äôs got amazing properties including being highly fire resistant. In the 2009 Kinglake fires in Victoria, Australia 183 lives were lost. People died when they were stranded in their cars trying to escape, from being burnt alive, from radiant heat and from suffocation. One man died about 100 metres from a safe evacuation zone from suffocation as the fire sucked all the oxygen out of the air. Kinglake is a mountainous area so the fire spread extremely quickly. It was a hot, extremely windy day.   Sadly sometimes Mother Nature is not very kind. And there wasn‚Äôt much that could have saved the 183 people who died from the ferocious fires  and winds on that day. Very, very sad.   The take away being, that , if you live in a dangerous fire zone always be prepared to evacuate on high risk days.   I live in the Perth Hills in Western Australia, one the highest risk fire zones in the world. We get 1000 mm of rain during winter and spring but we have long very hot, dry summers.   The Local Government does regular controlled burns during the cooler months. On many occasions this has saved houses and lives as the fire has hit a controlled burn zone and run out of fuel.   The power company checks all properties annually and issues notices to homeowners to trim trees that are close to power lines. If you don‚Äôt do it, within 30 days, they do it for you but charge you a fortune.  All property owners are required to create fire breaks around their properties to not only help prevent the spread of fire but also to allow access to fire trucks.   And everyone spends winter and spring burning off fallen branches and leaves. It‚Äôs a year long process but it works.

As an Australian Builder, we have a National Construction Code specifically for Bushfire affected zones called BAL (Bushfire Attack Level), the ratings are   BAL Low, BAL-12.5, BAL-19, BAL-29, BAL-40 and BAL-FZ ( Flame Zone) the restrictions / requirements get more complex and stringent the higher you go,   I'm surprised America does not have similar building codes for these susceptible areas, and feel after this tragic event there will be some revision of building codes, yes it does add extra cost to the build the extra protection, but if done right and maintained a house built to the particular code will have a lot better chance of surviving a bushfire than one built to regular codes, Interesting you mentioned TREX decking we have it AUS it only has a BAL rating of 19, or 29, so it is not allowed in BAL 40 or BAL FZ zones and would have never survived the LA fires, also at BAL 40 you can't have any timber joinery externally if you want timber windows, these have to be fitted with fireproof metal shutters, BAL-FZ you can't even have timber windows even with fire shutters.  Decks we build in these areas are all steel framed and cement fibre decking boards (no timber and no PVC composites) with FZ decks also requiring metal clip in strips between each board to completely seal the top surface, this is so no embers can fall through the boards, I think the USA can learn a lot from Australia's bushfire disasters and why we introduced these tough building standards for fire affected zones.

A more generally applicable example, from a very similar situation, occurred during the Marshall Fire in Louisville, Colorado. One section of newly built homes all survived when houses across the street (on 3 sides if I remember correctly) all burned down. They were constructed with cement fiber siding and without soffit/attic vents. This prevented the windblown embers from finding ingress.   The conditions and visuals are frighteningly familiar. There was no stopping the fire, there was only the race to evacuate as many as possible before the fire cut them off. I remember being glued to the local weather data praying for the wind speed to drop - and that it wouldn't turn towards us.

Homes rebuilt on top of each other on steep hillsides with narrow streets and inadequate water supply. We had just built a brand new water tank for EBMUD just months before the disaster.  13 people died within a few hundred feet of the new tank, trapped by the flames.  Tragically, about the only thing left standing was that water tank.  Why?  The idiot designers at EBMUD designed the valves to close whenever power went out. I live on the outskirts of Melbourne Australia and I am gob smacked at how CA houses have little defence. We are in a high fire risk zone and when we renovated our house (knockdown and rebuild) we had to meet several fire regulations. 1000L water storage tank that collect rain water with an attachment for fire engines, Steel Roofing (colour bond). We also used Hebel instead of brick which apparently repels heat better which help us meet the fire safety rating. Our State also conducts burn offs regularly. You cant stop the fires especially when people are lighting them (50% of bushfires in Oz are deliberately lit) but you can reduce the risks and improve defence,

You know, there's still a lingering 'disadvantage' to having your house survive a fire like this: the destroyed neighborhood surrounding it. When the entire neighborhood around you burns to the ground there's no community, no context, no beauty, and no social structure left. What remains is your house left sitting in a scene out of a smoking Armageddon. Suddenly your neighbors and your memories of them and of the neighborhood in the past are all in the past. Much of the foliage is burned away and gone. The familiarity is gone and what's left is a wrecked moonscape. The corner mall where you maybe did much of your regular shopping is probably gone too, as are various other former conveniences. The area is largely silent and dead around your surviving house.  So even for someone whose house survives if it is the only one nearby which did it's going to be its own kind of weird and isolated trauma coming back to that surviving house and WANTING to continue living there since it's now been translplanted into such wrecked and (at least initially) abandoned terrain. Imagine having no neighbors, no ordinary neighborhood traffic, no familiar neighborhood sights, sounds, and smells any more. Maybe there are even no birds around for a while, etc. And then, if you decided you couldn't stand continuing to live an an abandoned, burned-out mauseleum of a neighborhood bereft of its comforting surrouding environment and conveniences, imagine trying to SELL your surviving house to some buyer who would face the same stark circumstances upon moving in. I suppose the only selling point would be getting along the the neighbors is not a problem when there are none. And then too I wonder if a single house left standing in a burned-out neighborhood might not be a target for future crime due to its occupants' newfound isolation. There is no neighborhood watch factor any more when there are no neighbors, etc.

Retired UK Building Surveyor and Engineer here . Checked out your Building Regs ( Codes) and can only say that we have not built this way in the UK since 1666 when the Great Fire destroyed London . Even Class A construction would be un insurable in the UK .Timber frame construction is permissable here but the specs are much higher . Usually a render finish over metal lathing on 50mm mineral fibre insulation on a microporous fireproof wallboard , vapour barrier on timber stud or Seghal frame . One or 2 layers of Gyproc wallboard inside to achieve I hour internally . The product displayed is known here as expanded metal lathing by Expamet and available in galvanised or stainless Steel. Flat rooof dressing is known as " solar chippings ", usually of limestone used to protect 3 layer felt or bitumastic coatings .

Around here (British Columbia, Canada) both the fire departments and the public still seem fixated on FireSmarting homes. Nothing more. This involves removing combustibles from the perimeter and creating a 'defensible space'. What they don't understand is that a 'defensible space' only reduces risk and allows room for firefighters to work. It definitely helps to remove combustibles, but between ninety and approaching one hundred percent of all structure ignitions are caused by wind-blown firebrands (embers). If your home is not tight, if there is one little 2-3mm gap anywhere in the roof system or siding or crawl space, embers will find their way inside and ignite the nearest combustible materials. Without meticulous attention to these details your home *will* burn.
Modern homes in Germany are frequently built from a brick masonry formed of sand and limestone, along with asphalt roof tiles.  The thickness of load-bearing walls is not less than 150 mm, and partitions - 100 mm. Communication systems and wiring are mounted in protective boxes, in the middle of the panels.

I pretty much don't like that idea, because of water damage to the inside of the house.  The likely start of a fire would be inside the house.  The likely place of a fire would be the stove/ventalation area.  So why not a commerical sprinkler for over the stove.  Yes and installing those screens you mentioned. What about some type of external demister? Some cities use these to cool the area of a downtown. When people leave for a fire, turn on the demister (atomizes the water droplets the pulses every 1-2 min).  A pump that is on battery back up for when the electrical supply fails. A 4 day supply tank (size depends on size of house).  A layered defense, 1. immediate area around house, 2. the material used for roof and walls, 3.  external Demister/sprinkler for a house during forest fire. The one culprit of the fires nobody talks about is the invasive golden grass that covers the hills of California which were planted by early Spanish settlers for their livestock. Even if the forests were managed correctly you would still have issue with fires because the invasive grass is dry and highly flammable which makes it the primary source of fuel for the fires in places like the Pacific Palisades where there aren't dense forests. Native California grass aren't as flammable because their deep roots hold more moisture and native California tress also are resilient to fires that's why you see so many of them survive around homes that have burned down. The solution is to remove the invasive grass along with every invasive plant and tree species that aren't native to California, the work has to be divided between every California County and City.  Building codes need to be updated to require metal roofs on all structures. Key West has required Metal roofs on all structures the great fire of Key West in 1886. California's may find it worthwhile to look at how we operate in my home state of New South Wales Australia.   We fairly regularly have massive wild fires that can last for many months some dozens of fires spread across the whole state, often made worse by our highly combustible eucalyptus forests.   We have a full time professional fire fighting force - NSW Fire and Rescue with 6,800 fire-fighters PLUS 5,891 volunteer Community Fire Unit members. PLUS the state wide Rural Fire Service that has 72,000 almostüòÆ entirely volunteer fire fighters. The RFS is well equipped and trained and is set up to fight mainly bush (wild) fires.   Volunteer Community Fire Units are equipped by F&R with a small box trailer, petrol (gas) fire pumps (to use swimming pool water) fire hoses, personal protection gear (uniform, goggles, face mask, boots). Each CFU is set up to protect the volunteers own and neighbours homes, they aren't deployed outside their local home area.     The goal is for F&R to follow the fire front as it moves along, the role of the CFU is to stay put at their homes, dampen down and clear flammable materials around homes before the fire front arrives, after the fire front moves on the CFU puts out spot fires.     In Australia we understand in a massive fire that a fire engine can't be outside every home and we as local residents have to do stuff for ourselves, ideally via a CFU.     We know through experience that once 2 or 3 fire trucks plug into our streets fire hydrants water pressure plummets. That's why petrol fire pumps to use swimming pool water are so important.     Do you have equivalent of our CFU's in American?

I love that you mentioned the problem with vinyl windows in fire areas, because the replacement window industry is FILLED with companies that want to replace your old aluminum windows with vinyl.  Yes, they have options these days for special glass that reflects heat, etc.  But vinyl window frames can destroy your house if there is a fire nearby.  Special glass does no good if it's laying on the ground below your window!  We live in San Diego County in a fire prone area, and had our homeowner's insurance cancelled a few years ago.  We had to go with the California Fair Plan for fire insurance.  This is a high-risk insurance pool run by the state, but that and out supplemental (non-fire) premiums are actually lower than we were paying!  Fair plan rates your fire risk and charges accordingly.  We have their best rating because of all our defensive space.  They send out inspectors to verify that.    Everybody seems to be looking for someone to blame for the LA area fires, but most of that is ridiculous.  Governors do not control the weather!  The areas where the fires burned have no forests to "manage".  No leave litter to rake.  The native vegetation is all chaparral.  You could bulldoze those slopes, but then you would have horrific mud slides when it rains.  No amount of water squirted on your burning house is going to save it when you have 100 MPH gusts pushing the fire.  Fire departments and districts have been telling us how to make our homes more fire-resistant for years, but that's mostly ignored.  Most of the reckless disregard is ours.  Beyond that, I guess you could blame God for making Santa Ana winds.  We never had a fire disaster in January before.  I guess the climate scientists are right.  It's only going to get worse. I have a keen eye to notice the hedges around these surviving homes.    We think of plants as fuel for the fire, but plants are adapted to reflect heat.  It‚Äôs essential to their thermal regulation as a thing that sits in the sun all day.    Foliage is basically a mirror to infrared heat. So it shields whatever is behind it from the radiant heat.   It may be net protective, even though it dries out and then burns itself, but in many instances we see lots of unburned trees bushes and lawn in the aftermath of these fires.    The key is they have to be watered plants, and they also can‚Äôt be plants that have adapted to burn intentionally as part of their life cycle.   One way we can think about it, is how much thermal energy is release by a plant burning, as compared to how much thermal energy from an adjacent home is deflected by the plant. Wildfires typically have a disproportionate destructive impact on residential buildings when they occur. There are many factors that need to be addressed when looking at how to reduce the destruction experienced by houses during wildfires, and an unfortunate reality is that it is very difficult to sufficiently overcome the vulnerabilities in many existing homes, as their very design and construction is what makes them essentially fire tinder. New construction allows for design approaches to incorporate features more commonly found in non-residential projects, however, this does little to help the vast majority of homes. Designing for wildfires is relatively straightforward, as the design challenges being dealt with are known and predictable: combustion and heat management are the two factors that need to be addressed across the entire continuous building envelope. In LA, the Getty Museum is a great example of a project designed for wildfires. The project was designed from the outset with the goal of being able to shield art in-place. Materiality was a major part of the design, focusing on using non-combustible materials. A challenge in a country like the US, and no doubt others, is that into the future areas exposed to the risk of wildfires will continue to increase in number, and the dark reality is that homes that exist in these areas now, which will constitute the bulk of housing stock into this approaching future, don‚Äôt stand a chance against a wildfire. Homes in Massachusetts aren‚Äôt designed to withstand wildfires, yet we already experience them. Until we start to see adoption and implementation of more rigorous code requirements for residential construction, and we have programs aimed at helping homeowners retrofit existing homes, it‚Äôs difficult to see how this isn‚Äôt going to be something we see repeat itself over and over again.

I built a home in the north of San Diego County in the wild lands 5 years ago.  I acted as general contractor and did about 10-15% of the actual construction.  I designed the 2600 sq ft house  and researched many materials.  The last was of limited value because I hit a brick wall of "we have always done it this way" from subcontractors and inspectors.  Fortunately, S.D. Building and Safety has been constantly upgrading fire standards for years now.  My home was required to have a totally sealed attic.  The only openings are special (required) O'Hagin Fire and Ice roof vents .  Their website is educational as they have close-up scientific videos of how a wind driven firestorm forces embers into an attic.  Their vents are designed with both a fine mesh and a long internal serpentine pathway that give the embers time to die before passing through all the way.  Their videos show how effective these vents are under extreme test conditions.  You can imagine just how glad I am to have those in place.  As to mesh size, 1/4" has been shown to be an ember freeway.  1/8" is better but of dubious value.  Personally. I would not put 100% faith even in the 1/16" mesh without the serpentine pathway to extinguish the smallest embers.  Over the years, the standards have repeatedly tightened as it was discovered that the previous fixes just were not reliable.      Tempered glass is required in all windows as it is fire resistant.  My window frames have aluminum cladding. Bathroom vents are required.  Builders love to use flexible plastic or aluminum duct, but that seem to me to be vulnerable to embers and pests.  I insisted on steel wall 4" duct.  The code should require that IMHO and maybe it does now.  Ditto for the kitchen hood duct.  The exterior of my home is stucco, but that almost goes without saying in So Ca.        5' of defensible space?  Here they want 50'.  And I comply but my insurance has tripled here ($2500 to $7500).  Soon to go up (skyrocket) to cover for LA without a doubt.  Of course that assumes that the rest of the insurance companies don't just flee California.  $200 billion in losses (so far) is a pretty big pill to swallow.      I am going to mention that San Diego County and City and SD Gas and Electric should be commended for their highly proactive approach to fires.  Fires are jumped on incredibly swiftly and SDGE is constantly inspecting power lines with helicopters and otherwise and cutting back brush.  The opposite was true at the deadly Paradise fire in northern California.  After the fire, I saw pictures on Youtube of high voltage power lines hanging from brackets worn almost paper thin from many years of swaying in the breeze. A lot of people died from that neglect.       In case you are wondering about my attic temperatures with limited venting, here is the story.  The fiberglass shingles are class A and heat reflective.  The roof deck is OSB with aluminum clad to the underside for UV resistance. Under that is a 1" air gap and that is followed by 3-1/2" of of fiberglass insulation   There is also R38 fiberglass above the ceiling.  This does a very acceptable job of keeping the attic comfortable, but this is San Diego and the bar is set very low.  My home is is fairly well sealed, but only relative to the climate.  As it stands, my high efficiency heat pump sees minimal use and only under what we laughingly call extreme conditions and a lot of people would probably never turn it on.

I've survived a number of wild bush fires in a high bushfire attack area  The formula to protection is pretty simple 1. Make sure there is NO combustible material with-in yards of any buildings.  Pine bark, flower beds, bushes, stacked firewood, old cars, really just anything stored against the house.  Even if apparently non-combustible 'things' against the building can trap embers. 2. All external building materials must be non-combustible  (This specifically excludes plastic siding, plastic gutters and down spouts, plastic window frames, tar product roofing such as asphalt shingles.  It also excludes externally attached insulation.)  Consider using Hardie-board, tiles, brick, stone, cinder block, concrete, steel sheet roofing, tile roofing 3. Consider ember attack, that being burning embers and sparks blowing on to and into the building. Reduce inside corners that can catch embers or specifically line internal corners with sheet iron flashing, fill the gap between gutter and eves with gauze or hardy board to stop embers getting into the roof space.  Verandas are deadly catch points for embers so construct from non-flammables like cement, tile, pebbledash and remove all furniture and ember traps before the fire hits you. 4. For active protection  install a garden sprinkler system with gas/diesel powered pump and the largest rainwater tank/s you can fit.  Block your down pipes and fill the gutters with water and then set the irrigation to run just before you leave the property... and pray.
More than 20years ago I got a copy of "Fine Home Building".  On the back cover was a picture of a burnt out neighborhood of more than 100 homes that were all burnt to the ground.  In the middle of all of these completely destroyed homes was one home that survived without a scratch.  There was a story what the home owner did to his home while it was being built to protect it from wildfires.
A program I watched years ago on this subject explained that the heat of the fire is carried by the wind ahead of the fire.  That heat will heat up the wood on a building till it is almost to combustion temperature.  Then when the embers from the fire land on that wood, they are the trigger that causes the wood to start burning on contact and spread dozens of times faster than normal.  Do like Matt has shown before and clad the entire exterior of a building with rock wool insulation and then cover that with steel or fiber cement siding and roofing, have something like steel shutters that can cover and protect the windows, then the exterior of the building is all but fireproof.

From Bloomberg News:   ‚ÄúThe yard is a protected area free of vegetation, fenced off by cast-in-place concrete garden walls, with landscaping in a sparse Mediterranean desert style. The home‚Äôs owner has been¬†through fires before, so¬†he was prepared: He removed trash cans and other loose items from around the house and even left the side gates open, knowing that a fire can spread along a fence to a house.¬† ‚ÄúWe were unfortunate that the neighbor parked the car adjacent to the house. There‚Äôs molten aluminum in the picture, 1,200 degrees,‚Äù Chasen says.¬†‚ÄúThat wall prevented a lot of that heat from getting to the house.‚Äù Other design factors are more subtle. Along the side of the house there are no eaves or overhangs, which can form eddies or trap embers blown by high winds. The house doesn‚Äôt have any attic vents to allow sparks to get inside the roof, which is metal, with a fire-resistant underlayment. And the house is simple: front-gabled without multiple roof lines, dormers or other pop-outs, which are vulnerable intersections in a fire. Still other elements are invisible ‚Äî yet critical. The walls of the house have a one-hour fire rating. The deck is Class A wood, as resistant to ignition as concrete or steel, Chasen says. Tempered glass protects the interiors. And the front of the house was built with heat-treated wood, shielded from flying sparks and embers by the extruding walls and roof line.‚Äùhttps://apple.news/AYYWG4_DpSxa08fxB9MxltQ

Great video and I've known about this stuff for a long time - mostly from all the fires up in Northern California and many people who built homes with metal roofs, ember resistant decks and siding, and defensible space saw their house become "miracle homes".  Shutters are also great for windows especially if your house is right up against a neighbor and will get direct heat and flame when it burns.  I know people think they are ugly but you could even have emergency shutters that could be attached if you know a fire is coming.  I've also seen ridge sprinkler systems with independent water and power sources, and even ridge retracting roof covers for people who can't have a class A roof.  I'm also thinking some of the thermal break techniques you have will prevent heat being transmitted into a home which is important when there's combustible material on the inside. We really need huge incentives to build homes like these especially around perimeters and also to retrofit existing homes - just like we do for earthquakes.  That could either be via insurance rebates, or direct incentives.  Think about what even a fraction of the $200B in homes lost (so far) could have done if spent on preventative retrofitting.   And remember there's a herd immunity thing here - you don't need every single home to be fireproof - but every one added can help save a neighboring house which saves another and another and so on.  These homes in LA will probably cost high six figures if not in the millions to rebuild - surely tens of thousands of dollars in material and construction upgrades is worth that - especially if you get a discount on insurance for the lifetime of the home.

I've said from the beginning of this tragedy is that fire defensible construction should be mandatory for reconstruction, as well as new construction, in wildfire-prone areas (which the affected area is). It was a stick framed structure, completely wrapped in Densglass, but had was small spot where we could not wrap it completely. Thank you, Matt. I‚Äôm an architect and have lived in Southern California for 62 years. I spent my teen years in Pacific Palisades so I‚Äôll speak about that fire. I‚Äôm in no way saying it‚Äôs more important than Altadena. I am heartbroken that the small town of my youth is gone. Literally gone. Some added information about the winds for you. Firstly, Santa Ana winds are strong very dry winds that suck the moisture out of anything they touch. The second issue is called a mountain wave. In essence the strong winds striking a mountain at close to perpendicular race up the face like water up the back of a wave. It breaks over the top and rushes down the ‚Äúface of the wave‚Äù creating strong gusts. Take 60mph Santa Ana winds and amplify them by the mountain wave and you have 100mph dry gusts. This is what hit the palisades. This is also why fire burned downhill.  These measures you talk about will help greatly. But it is also luck of the draw.
